MERGE (prog3:`Program`{`id`:'P3',`name`:'BioSense Program',`fullName`:'BioSense Program',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'The BioSense Program was created by congressional mandate as part of the Public Health Security and Bioterrorism Preparedness and Response Act of 2002, and it was launched by the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) in 2003. BioSense is a near real-time surveillance system that receives and processes electronic healthcare encounter data, including, chief complaints, final diagnosis codes, procedure codes, clinical laboratory, pharmacy prescription, and patient demographic data from participating public health jurisdictions non-federal hospital emergency departments and inpatient facilities in addition to all United States Department of Defense (DoD) and Veterans Affairs (VA) outpatient hospitals and clinics nationwide. The BioSense Program also receives pharmacy data from a private sector health information exchange firm and laboratory data from two national level private sector clinical laboratories. The BioSense Program is in the process of transitioning from the original BioSense application to the BioSense 2.0 application that has new governance, a new organizational structure, and a new process for data submission and management. The Association of State and Territorial Health Officials (ASTHO) has been funded through a cooperative agreement with CDCs Division of Notifiable Disease and Healthcare Information (DNDHI) within the Public Health Surveillance and Informatics Program Office (PHSIPO) of the Office of Surveillance, Epidemiology, and Laboratory Services (OSELS) to facilitate the governance of BioSense 2.0, and through a contract with a vendor, ASTHO will offer access and use of BioSense 2.0 on a voluntary basis to state, local, and territorial health jurisdictions. Unlike the BioSense 1.0 application where participating organizations data were processed and stored at CDC in the CDC owned and operated Information Technology Services Offices Mid-Tier Data Center on secure servers, all data submitted by users in BioSense 2.0 will reside in a cloud-enabled, web-based platform that sits in the secure, private Government Cloud and is in compliance with the Federal Information Security Management Act. The platform will provide users with an exclusive secure space as well as tools for posting, receiving, controlling, analyzing, and sharing their public health surveillance information with other public health jurisdictions, CDC, or other public health partners. The public health jurisdiction will retain ownership of any data it contributes to its exclusive secure space within BioSense 2.0.',`webResource`:'http://www.cdc.gov/BioSense/',`operationalStatus`:'Partially Operational and Implemented',`startDate`:'',`startDateGeneration`:'2003',`surveillanceType`:'',`surveillanceDomain`:'',`conditionsUnderSurveillance`:'',`geographicCoverageArea`:'',`patientTrackingCapability`:'',`overallCapability`:'',`ageFormat`:'',`specialPopulations`:'',`appilicationSoultionType`:'',`applicationPlatform`:'',`operatingSystemPlatform`:'',`databaseTechnology`:'',`dataPayloadFormat`:'',`dataPayloadTransmissionType`:'',`dataParticipantType`:'',`analysis,Visualization,AndReporting(AVR)Capability`:'',`reportFrequency`:'',`evaluationDate`:'',`evaluationDetails`:'',`fundingSource`:'',`fundingLevels`:'',`nationalBiosurveillanceStrategyMapping`:'',`city`:'',`state`:'',`contactName`:'',`contactEmail`:'',`contactPhone`:'',`Notes`:''}) return prog3;

MERGE (prog14:`Program`{`id`:'P14',`name`:'Global Disease Detection Program',`fullName`:'Global Disease Detection Program',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'The Global Disease Detection (GDD) program is CDCs principal and most comprehensive program for developing and strengthening global capacity to rapidly detect, accurately identify, and promptly contain emerging infectious disease and bioterrorist threats that occur internationally. GDD centers work closely with the subject matter experts at CDC and with the host countries on six core capacities that include Emerging Infectious Disease Detection and Response, Training in field epidemiology and laboratory methods, pandemic influenza preparedness and response, zoonotic disease research and containment at human-animal interface, health communications, and laboratory systems and biosafety. MERGE (prog18:`Program`{`id`:'P18',`name`:'HIV Drug Resistance',`fullName`:'HIV Drug Resistance',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'Surveillance of transmitted HIV drug resistance (HIVDR) and HIVDR emerging during treatment and also of antiretroviral treatment (ART) program factors associated with the emergence/prevention of HIVDR during treatment. Countries form HIVDR working groups generally based at the Ministry of Health. There are five surveillance elements: 1. HIVDR Early Warning Indicators (EWI) -- all ART sites, or representative ART sites, are surveyed annually for factors associated with HIVDR emergence/prevention: A. ART prescribing practices; B. Percentage of patients lost to follow-up after one year following ART start C. Retention on first-line ART at one year; D. Percentage of ART patients picking up their drugs on-time; E. Percentage of ART patients keeping their appointments on time F. Percentage of months in which there are no ART drug stock-outs. Sample size is calculated based on numbers of ART patients at the site. Targets are recommended; countries may select more stringent targets. 2. Monitoring of HIVDR prevention and associated factors at sentinel ART sites: HIVDR working groups select three - 15 sentinel sites annually and do baseline genotypes and endpoint genotypes on patients starting ART, and abstract other information from medical records associated with HIVDR prevention. 3. HIVDR transmission surveys: These surveys are each focused in one geographic area of the country and use a binominal sequential sampling technique which classifies prevalence of transmitted drug resistance for each of the three ART drug classes used in resource-limited countries as above or below two thresholds: 5% and 15%. 4. Cross-sectional surveys: A representative cross-section of ART patients on first-line ART are surveyed for viral load, and if the viral load is detectable (=ART failure), the specimen is genotyped to evaluate the resistance pattern. 5. Pediatric surveillance of initial HIVDR in children < =18 months of age: Remnant dried blood spots used for pediatric HIV diagnosis are genotyped for children who are HIV-positive, prevention-of-mother to child transmission (PMTCT) information is collected (which regimens received by child and mother) to evaluate resistance patterns associated with PMTCT and also for those children whose PMTCT status is recorded as \"unknown\". Information is used for public health action to change ART program practices at site and national level. The program also includes HIVDR prevention elements (training), a database into which survey data can be entered, a lab training and accreditation system for surveillance genotyping.',`webResource`:'http://www.who.int/hiv/topics/drugresistance/en/index.html',`operationalStatus`:'Fully Operational and Implemented',`startDate`:'',`startDateGeneration`:'2005',`surveillanceType`:'',`surveillanceDomain`:'',`conditionsUnderSurveillance`:'',`geographicCoverageArea`:'',`patientTrackingCapability`:'',`overallCapability`:'',`ageFormat`:'',`specialPopulations`:'',`appilicationSoultionType`:'',`applicationPlatform`:'',`operatingSystemPlatform`:'',`databaseTechnology`:'',`dataPayloadFormat`:'',`dataPayloadTransmissionType`:'',`dataParticipantType`:'',`analysis,Visualization,AndReporting(AVR)Capability`:'',`reportFrequency`:'',`evaluationDate`:'',`evaluationDetails`:'',`fundingSource`:'',`fundingLevels`:'',`nationalBiosurveillanceStrategyMapping`:'',`city`:'',`state`:'',`contactName`:'',`contactEmail`:'',`contactPhone`:'',`Notes`:''}) return prog18;

MERGE (ssys20:`SurveillanceSystem`{`id`:'SS20',`name`:'CaliciNet',`fullName`:'',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'CaliciNet, pioneered by CDCs Division of Viral Diseases, National Center for Immunization and Respiratory Diseases, is a nationwide electronic surveillance system of local and state public health and regulatory agency laboratories that will help scientists solve mysteries about norovirus disease outbreaks. CaliciNet went live March 16, 2009, connecting state and local public health laboratories across the United States; information gleaned from the network will provide a better understanding of noroviruses and help control their spread. Worldwide, noroviruses are the main cause of foodborne diarrheal disease, accounting for more cases of gastroenteritis than all bacterial causes combined. About 1 in 13 Americans becomes ill with norovirus gastroenteritis every year. CaliciNet also has the potential to help identify possible links between clusters of norovirus illness and common-source exposures. CaliciNet uses reverse transcriptionpolymerase chain reaction followed by DNA sequencing of partial regions of the norovirus genome. Unlike most birth defects, where the causes are unknown, the etiology of FAS (prenatal exposure to alcohol) is known. FAS is 100% preventable if a woman does not drink alcohol during pregnancy. Despite increasing efforts to prevent alcohol exposed pregnancies, children continue to be born with disabilities resulting from prenatal alcohol exposure. Studies by the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) indicate FAS rates ranging from 0.2 to 1.5 per 1,000 live births in different areas of the United States. Other studies have estimated the rate of FAS at 0.5 to 2.0 cases per 1,000 live births. The variation in rates of FAS is likely a reflection of variation in study populations, case definition, case ascertainment sources, and surveillance methodologies among studies. CDC has been funding state health departments to establish or enhance population-based surveillance of FAS since 1997. As a result of these efforts, a standardized, multiple-source surveillance methodology was developed and implemented by the funded states. Currently, CDC funds three FAS surveillance programs located in Arizona, Colorado, and New York. MERGE (ssys59:`SurveillanceSystem`{`id`:'SS59',`name`:'HIV Incidence Surveillance',`fullName`:'',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'HIV incidence is defined as the rate of new HIV infections over a defined period of time. HIV incidence can be used to appropriately target prevention resources to those most vulnerable to HIV transmission, identify masked or missed sub-epidemics, closely monitor for the emergence of new sub-epidemics of HIV in the general population, and measure the impact of HIV prevention programs. Several approaches have been used for measuring HIV incidence, yet each presents specific challenges. These main approaches include direct measurement in cohort studies, inference from HIV prevalence measurements in cross-sectional surveys using mathematical models, and a laboratory-based approach using tests for recent infection (TRI) in cross-sectional surveys. TRIs can be used to estimate national HIV-1 incidence in general populations in resource-constrained settings by applying the assay to specimens derived from national population based surveys with HIV testing (e.g. Demographic Health Surveys + and AIDS Indicator Surveys). Temporal trends in national HIV-1 incidence estimates can be subsequently assessed in countries that apply the assay in two or more consecutive rounds of national population based surveys. Temporal trends in HIV-1 incidence among target populations can also be tracked by applying the TRI to populations with three or more rounds of HIV sentinel surveillance. Finally, impact of PEPFAR supported HIV prevention programming can be measured using the TRI assay, through application of the assay to the targeted population at various cross-sectional points in time, including pre and post prevention programming.',`webResource`:'https://www.pepfar.net/C7/C8/HIV%20Incidence/default.aspx',`operationalStatus`:'Partially Operational and Implemented',`startDate`:'',`startDateGeneration`:'1985',`surveillanceType`:'',`surveillanceDomain`:'',`conditionsUnderSurveillance`:'',`geographicCoverageArea`:'',`patientTrackingCapability`:'',`overallCapability`:'',`ageFormat`:'',`specialPopulations`:'',`appilicationSoultionType`:'',`applicationPlatform`:'',`operatingSystemPlatform`:'',`databaseTechnology`:'',`dataPayloadFormat`:'',`dataPayloadTransmissionType`:'',`dataParticipantType`:'',`analysis,Visualization,AndReporting(AVR)Capability`:'',`reportFrequency`:'',`evaluationDate`:'',`evaluationDetails`:'',`fundingSource`:'',`fundingLevels`:'',`nationalBiosurveillanceStrategyMapping`:'',`city`:'',`state`:'',`contactName`:'',`contactEmail`:'',`contactPhone`:'',`Notes`:''}) return ssys59;

Medical chart abstraction occurs for each hospitalized case meeting the surveillance case definition. The IHSP began during the 2009-2010 season to enhance surveillance during the 2009 H1N1 pandemic.  IHSP sites included IA, ID, MI, OK and SD during 2009-2010 season; ID, MI, OH, OK, RI, and UT during the 2010-2011 season; MI, OH, RI, and UT during the 2011-2012 season; and IA, MI, OH, RI, and UT during the 2012-2013 season. Data collected through this platform is used to monitor influenza disease severity and estimate influenza disease burden. Dissemination of health-related data collected through FluSurv-NET is disseminated in FluView, with periodic updates on influenza-associated hospitalization rates by age group. Incidence rates are calculated using the National Center for Health Statistics (NCHS) population estimates for the counties included in the surveillance catchment area.  The rates provided are likely to be a vast underestimate of the actual number of influenza-related hospitalizations. MERGE (ssys66:`SurveillanceSystem`{`id`:'SS66',`name`:'Internally Displaced Persons Surveillance System - Haiti',`fullName`:'',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'On January 12, 2010, a 7.0-magnitude earthquake in Haiti disrupted infrastructure and displaced approximately 2 million persons, causing increased risk for communicable diseases from overcrowding and poor living conditions. Hundreds of nongovernmental organizations (NGOs) established health-care clinics in camps of internally displaced persons (IDPs). To monitor conditions of outbreak potential identified at NGO camp clinics, on February 18, the Haiti Ministry of Public Health and Population (MSPP), the Pan-American Health Organization (PAHO), and CDC implemented the IDP Surveillance System (IDPSS). The Inter-Agency Standing Committee (IASC) \"cluster approach\" was used to coordinate the Haiti humanitarian response. One of 11 clusters, the Global Health Cluster (GHC), builds global capacity, whereas the country-level cluster (in this case, the Haitian Health Cluster [HHC], led by PAHO) responds locally. During the Haiti response, HHC engaged NGOs serving large camps, established IDPSS, followed trends of reportable conditions, undertook epidemiologic and laboratory investigations, and fostered implementation of control measures. This report describes the design and implementation of IDPSS in the post-earthquake period. The primary challenges to implementing IDPSS were communication difficulties with an ever-changing group of NGO partners and limitations to the utility of IDPSS data because of lack of reliable camp population denominator estimates. The IDPSS experience reinforces the need to improve local communication and coordination strategies. Improving future humanitarian response requires advance development and distribution of easily adaptable standard surveillance tools, development of an interdisciplinary strategy for an early and reliable population census, and development of communication strategies using locally available Internet and cellular networks.',`webResource`:'Activity website not reported',`operationalStatus`:'Fully Operational and Implemented',`startDate`:'',`startDateGeneration`:'2010',`surveillanceType`:'',`surveillanceDomain`:'',`conditionsUnderSurveillance`:'',`geographicCoverageArea`:'',`patientTrackingCapability`:'',`overallCapability`:'',`ageFormat`:'',`specialPopulations`:'',`appilicationSoultionType`:'',`applicationPlatform`:'',`operatingSystemPlatform`:'',`databaseTechnology`:'',`dataPayloadFormat`:'',`dataPayloadTransmissionType`:'',`dataParticipantType`:'',`analysis,Visualization,AndReporting(AVR)Capability`:'',`reportFrequency`:'',`evaluationDate`:'',`evaluationDetails`:'',`fundingSource`:'',`fundingLevels`:'',`nationalBiosurveillanceStrategyMapping`:'',`city`:'',`state`:'',`contactName`:'',`contactEmail`:'',`contactPhone`:'',`Notes`:''}) return ssys66;

MERGE (ssys87:`SurveillanceSystem`{`id`:'SS87',`name`:'National Biomonitoring Program',`fullName`:'',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'CDC\'s Environmental Health Laboratory operates the National Biomonitoring Program (NBP). Throughout the world, biomonitoring has become the standard for assessing people\'s exposure to toxic substances as well as for responding to serious environmental public health problems. Rather than estimating how much of a substance gets into people from measured environmental concentrations, our scientists have taken out the guesswork by measuring levels of chemicals that actually are in people\'s bodies. And they do so with precision, speed, and pinpoint accuracy, measuring many chemicals in a very small amountoften a teaspoon or lessof blood or urine. However, between October 2000 and September 2004, the CDC received reports of 50 patients with severe adverse events associated with the use of the two or three-month regimen of rifampin and pyrazinamide (RZ) for the treatment of LTBI; 12 (24%) patients died (Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report 2003;52[31]:735-9). A severe adverse event is defined as hospitalization or death of a person receiving treatment for LTBI. On the basis of these data, the American Thoracic Society and CDC recommended that RZ should generally not be offered for treatment of persons with LTBI, regardless of HIV status. Rifampin and pyrazinamide should continue to be administered in multidrug regimens for the treatment of persons with active TB disease. Reports of severe adverse events related to RZ and other older LTBI regimens prompted a need for this projecta national surveillance system of such events. The objective of the project is to determine the annual number and temporal trends of severe adverse events (hospitalization or death) associated with any treatment for LTBI in the United States. Surveillance of such events provide data to support periodic evaluation of guidelines for treatment of persons with LTBI and revision, as needed. This project is a systematic reporting system for severe adverse events (death or hospitalization) associated with therapy for LTBI. The system will rely on medical chart review of already existing data by TB control staff. Potential respondents are any of the 60 reporting areas for the national TB surveillance system (the 50 states, the District of Columbia, New York City, Puerto Rico, and 8 jurisdictions in the Pacific and Caribbean). Data will be collected using the data collection form for adverse events associated with LTBI treatment. CDC conducts site investigations, analyze and periodically publish reports summarizing national LTBI treatment adverse events statistics and also will conduct special analyses for publication in peer-reviewed scientific journals to further describe and interpret these data. PRAMS collects state-specific, population-based data on maternal attitudes and experiences before, during, and shortly after pregnancy. PRAMS was initiated in 1987 because infant mortality rates were no longer declining as rapidly as they had in prior years. In addition, the incidence of low birth weight infants had changed little in the previous 20 years. Research has indicated that maternal behaviors during pregnancy may influence infant birth weight and mortality rates. The goal of the PRAMS project is to improve the health of mothers and infants by reducing adverse outcomes such as low birth weight, infant mortality and morbidity, and maternal morbidity. PRAMS provides state-specific data for planning and assessing health programs and for describing maternal experiences that may contribute to maternal and infant health. PRAMS provides data for state health officials to use to improve the health of mothers and infants. PRAMS allows CDC and the states to monitor changes in maternal and child health indicators (e.g., unintended pregnancy, prenatal care, breast-feeding, smoking, drinking, infant health). PRAMS enhances information from birth certificates used to plan and review state maternal and infant health programs. The PRAMS sample is chosen from all women who had a live birth recently, so findings can be applied to the state\'s entire population of women who have recently delivered a live-born infant. Additionally, it contributes to the knowledge base that supports the development and dissemination of appropriate public health messages and educational materials for the general public and health care providers, as well as informing federal and state policy. In 2012, the CDC Office of Public Health Genomics developed a three-tiered framework for classifying genomic tests and family health history based on the availability of scientific evidence and evidence-based recommendations supporting their use. Tier 1 genomic applications are recommended for clinical use by evidence-based panels, based on systematic review of evidence of validity and utility. Tier 2 applications may be useful for informed decision making in clinical practice, based on demonstrated validity, and promising utility.  Tier 3 applications are not ready for clinical use, due to validity or utility not demonstrated, or systematic assessment finding harms outweigh benefits. States are assessing awareness and use of specific Tier 1, 2 and 3 genomic applications using state-added questions in the Behavioral Risk Factor Surveillance System (BRFSS). Ethical, legal and social issues:  The potential for genetic discrimination is a concern relevant to the increased availability of genetic information associated with the increased use of genetic tests in clinical practice and in the direct-to-consumer market.  The Genetic Information Nondiscrimination Act (GINA) was signed into law in 2008. While GINA is designed to protect against discrimination in health insurance coverage and employment, it does not address other areas where discrimination is possible, such as in life, disability and long-term care insurance. More information is needed on the extent that the general public is aware of protections offered under GINA, and is concerned about the potential for genetic discrimination, since these may prove to be significant factors affecting the uptake of genetic testing. MERGE (ssys152:`SurveillanceSystem`{`id`:'SS152',`name`:'Surveillance for Trichinellosis',`fullName`:'',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'Trichinellosis became a nationally notifiable disease in 1966, but statistics have been kept on the disease since 1947. The national trichinellosis surveillance system is a passive system that relies on existing resources at the local, state, and federal levels. Cases are diagnosed based on clinical history with laboratory confirmation of Trichinella infection. Cases are reported weekly to CDC through the National Electronic Telecommunications System for Surveillance (NETSS). The goal of the tool is to assist public health professionals in the early identification and monitoring of outbreaks of disease, as well as other public health and medical events, with emphasis on outbreaks that may indicate intentional releases of biological agents. The EARS tool facilitates creation of user-identified event/syndrome categories by which data are aggregated. Aberration detection algorithms then assess whether observed counts or rates for a particular category are higher than usual. MERGE (tool16:`Tool`{`id`:'TL16',`name`:'Exposure Investigation',`fullName`:'Exposure Investigation',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'An exposure investigation is one approach ATSDR uses to develop better characterization of past, current, and possible future human exposures to hazardous substances in the environment and to evaluate existing and possible health effects related to those exposures more thoroughly. There are three main ways information is gathered during an exposure investigation: Bio-medical testing (for example, urine or blood samples) is an important piece of information gathered and evaluated during an ATSDR exposure investigation. Bio-medical samples can show current (and sometimes past) exposure to a contaminant. Environmental testing (for contamination of soil, water, or air) is also an important piece of information gathered and evaluated during an exposure investigation. ATSDR investigators focus environmental testing on where people live, spend time and play, or might come in contact with contaminants under investigation. Our branch also has expertise in exposure dose reconstruction. Exposure-dose reconstruction analyses use environmental sampling information and computer models to estimate the contaminant levels that people may have been exposed to in the past or may be exposed to in the future. We can use these models to draw various conclusions. For example, how long and at what levels a drinking water well has been contaminated. We can then use the information to evaluate how a person\'s health might be affected.',`webResource`:'NULL',`operationalStatus`:'Retired',`startDate`:'',`startDateGeneration`:'NULL',`surveillanceType`:'',`surveillanceDomain`:'',`conditionsUnderSurveillance`:'',`geographicCoverageArea`:'',`patientTrackingCapability`:'',`overallCapability`:'',`ageFormat`:'',`specialPopulations`:'',`appilicationSoultionType`:'',`applicationPlatform`:'',`operatingSystemPlatform`:'',`databaseTechnology`:'',`dataPayloadFormat`:'',`dataPayloadTransmissionType`:'',`dataParticipantType`:'',`analysis,Visualization,AndReporting(AVR)Capability`:'',`reportFrequency`:'',`evaluationDate`:'',`evaluationDetails`:'',`fundingSource`:'',`fundingLevels`:'',`nationalBiosurveillanceStrategyMapping`:'',`city`:'',`state`:'',`contactName`:'',`contactEmail`:'',`contactPhone`:'',`Notes`:''}) return tool16;

PRAMS was initiated in 1987 because infant mortality rates were no longer declining as rapidly as they had in prior years. In addition, the incidence of low birth weight infants had changed little in the previous 20 years. Research has indicated that maternal behaviors during pregnancy may influence infant birth weight and mortality rates. The goal of the PRAMS project is to improve the health of mothers and infants by reducing adverse outcomes such as low birth weight, infant mortality and morbidity, and maternal morbidity. PRAMS provides state-specific data for planning and assessing health programs and for describing maternal experiences that may contribute to maternal and infant health. MERGE (reg10:`Registry`{`id`:'RG10',`name`:'National Select Agent Registry',`fullName`:'National Select Agent Registry',`shortName`:'',`registryType`:'',`purpose`:'The 2002 Bioterrorism Preparedness & Response Act requires entities to register with the U.S. Department of Health & Human Services (HHS) or Agriculture (USDA) if they possess, use, or transfer Select Biological Agents (SA) or Toxins that could pose a severe threat to public health and safety, animal/plant health, or animal/plant products. The 2002 Act (Public Law 107-188) requires maintenance of a national database of entities that are registered for possession of SA & toxins, laboratory compliance with national safety guidelines, security measures including controlled access to SA & toxins, security screening of entities and personnel, and criminal/civil penalties for inappropriate use of SA/toxins. NSAR is a joint CDC & USDA Animal and Plant Health Inspection Service (APHIS) program designed to control, protect, store & oversee the use, possession & transfer of SA & toxins which resulted from an OMB directive for the USDA and CDC to collaborate. NSAR maintains information regarding an entitys SA registration, lists SA/toxins they possess, records individual access & laboratory inspection information, and lists where SA work is conducted. Access & accurate processing of SA data via the NSAR system is intended to enable agencies to increase their ability to enforce and prevent the spread of infectious diseases and illnesses. Individuals desiring to conduct research related activities are screened and approved by Department of Justice\'s Criminal Justice Information Services. NSAR supports PMA E-Gov by minimizing data collection/entry redundancy, improving information sharing, and is expected to reduce the entities reporting burden when forms are submitted electronically. As a result of a comprehensive Operational Assessment, the CDC, APHIS and other key stakeholders have determined that the NSAR investment, in its current form, does not have the functionality and technical capabilities necessary to support the business and strategic goals of the organization. HSPD-21 defines biosurveillance as the process of active data-gathering with appropriate analysis and interpretation of biosphere data that might relate to disease activity and threats to human or animal healthwhether infectious, toxic, metabolic, or otherwise, and regardless or intentional or natural originin order to achieve early warning of health threats, early detection of health events, and overall situational awareness of disease activity. The Department of Health and Human Services (HHS) was charged with the responsibility of enhancing biosurveillance for human health and delegated this responsibility to CDC. In 2008, CDC established the Biosurveillance Coordination unit (BC) to oversee the necessary activities to address these mandates. The NBSHH established priorities for the nations next-generation biosurveillance capability to provide timely, comprehensive, and accessible information to strengthen public health practice, provide value to clinicians, and build upon current systems and resources. The Rapid Onset of Gastroenteritis with Unexplained Etiology (ROGUE) working group investigates foodborne diseases that may have a chemical etiology. Since the most common foodborne illnesses are due to biological organisms, these are much more commonly considered early in an outbreak. Recognition of chemical etiologies is often not made until later, and this hinders ultimate identification of responsible agents because of the timeliness with which samples must be collected. Better clues are needed to help clinicians and public health officials determine if a foodborne outbreak may have a chemical etiology. People that become ill from chemical contaminants in food may comment that the food had an unusual taste, smell or appearance. These organoleptic qualities can be important clues in identifying the types of agents that may have contaminated the food and caused illness, as well as helpful in identifying that a chemical induced foodborne illness may be occurring. Unfortunately, in the investigation of foodborne illness complaints, this type of information is seldom collected in a uniform manner.',`operationStartDate`:'NULL',`operationalStatus`:'Retired',`collaborativeParticipants`:'NULL',`surveillanceDomain`:'',`conditionsUnderSurveillance`:'',`geographicCoverageArea`:'',`contactName`:'',`contactEmail`:''}) return colla24;

MERGE (colla28:`Collaborative`{`id`:'CO28',`name`:'WHO Collaborating Center for Rotavirus and the Agents of Viral Gastroenteritis',`fullName`:'WHO Collaborating Center for Rotavirus and the Agents of Viral Gastroenteritis',`shortName`:'',`purpose`:'CDCs - Rotavirus Program serves as a World Health Organization (WHO) Collaborating Center for rotavirus and other viral agents of gastroenteritis. Globally, rotavirus is the cause of about  450,000 diarrheal deaths every year,  almost all of which occur in  developing countries. In addition, many more children will get dehydrated and require medical intervention and hospitalization. Several vaccines against rotavirus have been, or are being, developed to reduce this burden. Since 2006, two live attenuated vaccines against rotavirus have been licensed in more than 100 countries around the world and are now recommended by WHO for global use. One component of efforts to introduce rotavirus vaccines globally is the derivation of global, regional and local disease burden and strain prevalence estimates to help make the case for vaccination and to support countries decisions about rotavirus vaccine introduction.. The CDC and WHO have developed a generic protocol for standardized surveillance of rotavirus disease burden and strain prevalence, and with support from the Global Alliance for Vaccines and Immunization (GAVI) and in collaboration with CDC and country partners, WHO has recently developed a Global Rotavirus Surveillance Network made up of regional networks (about 60 countries) in the six WHO regions (Americas, Africa, European, Eastern Mediterranean, Southeast Asia and Western Pacific Regions) to collect current data.  The role of the CDC Rotavirus Program and its Collaborating Center is to provide the WHO with technical expertise and laboratory and epidemiologic support to aid in network activities. Another Rotavirus Collaborating Center from Melbourne, Australia and Rotavirus Reference Laboratories from the six WHO regions provide region-specific laboratory support. Terms of reference of the center: 1. To conduct evaluations of rotavirus vaccines in collaboration with the Department of Vaccines and Biologicals (V&B, WHO) and developing country investigators. 2. To conduct rotavirus disease and strain surveillance with support from the Childhood Vaccine Program, Program of Appropriate Technology in Health (PATH) of the Bill and Melinda Gates Foundation, and V&B, WHO 3. To serve as a vaccine evaluation training center for scientists for developing and developed countries. 4. To provide assistance in characterization and referencing of viral strains from WHO supported studies. 5. To provide reference strains and assistance for implementation of new diagnostic methods in developing countries for serogrouping and serotyping of the local viral strains.',`operationStartDate`:'NULL',`operationalStatus`:'Fully Operational and Implemented',`collaborativeParticipants`:'http://apps.who.int/whocc/Detail.aspx?cc_ref=USA-292&cc_contact=gentsch&',`surveillanceDomain`:'',`conditionsUnderSurveillance`:'',`geographicCoverageArea`:'',`contactName`:'',`contactEmail`:''}) return colla28;
